Search for top squark production in fully-hadronic final
states in proton-proton collisions at 13 TeV.
J. Dittmann1 , K. Hatakeyama1 , J. Pastika1 , C. Smith1 , J. Wilson1 , K. Mandal2 , G. Pasztor2 ,
D. Elvira3 , D. Dash4 , S. Swain4 , K. Ecklund5 , M. Kilpatrick5 , L. Gouskos6 , J. Incandela6 ,
H. Qu6 , R. Cavanaugh7 , A.H. Merritt7 , H. Wang7 , Z. Wu7 , S. Malik8 , S. Norberg8 , and
A. Rosado-Trinidad8
1 Baylor

University
Lórand University
3 Fermi National Accelerator Laboratory
4 National Institute of Science Education and Research, Bhubaneswar
5 Rice University
6 University of California, Santa Barbara
7 University of Illinois at Chicago
8 University of Puerto Rico
2 Eötvös

Abstract
A search for supersymmetry is presented based on proton-proton collision events
containing identified hadronically decaying top quarks, no leptons, and an imbalance pmiss
in transverse momentum. The data were collected with the CMS detector
T
at the CERN LHC at a center-of-mass energy of 13 TeV, and correspond to an integrated luminosity of 137 fb−1 . Search regions are defined in terms of the multiplicity
of bottom quark jet, top quark and W jet candidates, the pmiss
T , and the scalar sum
of jet transverse momenta. The targeted signal production scenarios are direct and
gluino-mediated top squark production as described in several simplified models.

2

Introduction

The standard model (SM) of particle physics has proven to be remarkably robust. Nonetheless,
the SM has well-known shortcomings, such as an instability in the calculation of the Higgs boson mass known as the fine-tuning (or hierarchy) problem. Supersymmetry (SUSY) is a widely
considered extension of the SM that introduces an additional symmetry of nature between
fermions and bosons. A new supersymmetric particle (sparticle) is proposed for each SM particle, with the same mass and quantum numbers but with a spin that differs by a half-integer
unit. For example, squarks are the SUSY partners of quarks. Divergent quantum corrections
to the Higgs boson mass due to virtual SM particles are cancelled by corresponding contributions from virtual sparticles, thus resolving the fine-tuning problem. Although the symmetry
proposed by SUSY cannot be exact, as no sparticles have yet been observed, the stabilising
features of SUSY can survive with a modest amount of fine tuning if sparticles are not much
heavier than their SM counterparts. In particular, the mass difference between a particle and
its corresponding sparticle should not be too large, in order for SUSY to provide a so-called
natural solution to the fine-tuning problem. Thus the SUSY partners of top quarks, the top
squarks, might have masses below or around the TeV scale and be accessible at the LHC.
This note describes a search for production of supersymmetric partner of top quark (top squark)
in fully-hadronic final states with the full Run 2 data collected by the CMS experiment. The
targeted production scenarios of top squark are direct top squark pair production and gluinomediated production of top squark in which top squarks are produced in cascade decays of
gluinos, supersymmetric partners of gluios. The search strategy for top squark production has
evolved over several rounds of analyses performed in the past, and two most recent analyses performed on 36.0 fb−1 of Run 2 data by CMS were published in [1, 2]. The basic search
strategy used in the analysis reported in this paper is inherited from that used in the previous publications; however, many improvements are made. The major analysis improvements
include:

• The triggers have been updated to reflect the unprescaled triggers during the full
data-taking period.
• The deepAK8 [3, 4] is employed for identifying boosted top quarks and W bosons,
and deep resolved top tagger [5, 6] is developed and employed for identifying top
quarks at low to intermidiate pT range.
• The deepCSV b-tagging algorithm [7] has been used to identify b-tagged jets instead
of the CSV v2-algorithm.
• The search region definitions are similar to those used in Refs. [1, 8] but higher top
and b quark multiplicity bins are introduced to enhance sensitivites to some gluinomediated top squark production scenarios as done in Refs. [2, 9] and search bins
are generally shifted to tighter regions to take advantage of the larger integrated
luminosities.

Datasets and simulated event samples
Standard model MC samples

Several CMSSW releases were used to process the SM Monte Carlo (MC) samples. The 2016
samples were reconstructed mainly in 9 4 X (RunIISummer16MiniAODv3). The 2017 MC samples were reconstructed in a 9 4 X (RunIIFall17MiniAODv2) release while the 2018 were reconstructed in a 10 2 X (RunIIAutumn18MiniAOD) release. The SM samples are listed in Tables

4

Object and event selection

194

This analysis has adapted the new NanoAOD ntuple format from CMS. NanoAOD format
contains the per-event information that is needed in most generic analyses. Most of the physics
objects are maintained by the corresponding POGs. An extension of the v3 NanoAOD format
was added for the inputs needed for DeepResolved top quark taggers and soft-b jet tagging.
The detail algorithms of these objects are described in below sessions.

195

4.1

190
191
192
193

196
197
198
199
200
201
202
203
204

Muon

Since this is all hadronic analysis, we veto loose ID muons which have pT > 5 GeV, |η | < 2.4 and
pass mini-isolation. Mini-isolation is defined as the ratio of the scalar sum of the PF candidates’
pT (from PF charged hadrons, neutral hadrons, and photons) in a cone of radius ∆R around the
lepton over the lepton’s pT , with corrections for contributions from pileup interactions [10]. The
cone size varies with the lepton’s pT . For pT < 50 GeV, ∆R = 0.2, for pT > 200 GeV, ∆R = 0.05,
and for 50 < pT < 200 GeV, ∆R = 10 GeV/pT . For muons, the value of the mini-isolation is
required to be less than 0.2.
Medium ID muon is also used in the trigger efficiency measurement in Section 5, Z invisible
background for the normalization factor in Section 7 and tt̄Z normalization factor in Section 9.

209

Selected muons are further corrected by the ID scale factor provided by the Muon POG [11],
and mini-isolation scale factor from the SUSY group [12]. The statistical and systematic uncertainties assocaited with scale factors are propogated. Systematic uncertainty from the tag-andprode methd, are estimated by varying the isolation cut on the tag muon, with variations of the
signaland background pdfs, and enlarging and reducing the mass window propogated.

210

4.2

205
206
207
208

211
212
213
214
215
216

Electron

We use veto ID electrons with pT > 5 GeV and |η | < 2.5. The difference of the η requirements
between electrons and muons comes from that the endcap of the muon detector ends at |η | =
2.4 while the endcap of the tracker ends around |η | = 2.5. Similarly to muons, electrons are
also required to pass the mini-isolation, with a value less than 0.1.
Medium ID electron is also used in the trigger efficiency measurement in Section 5, Z invisible
background for the normalization factor in Section 7 and tt̄Z normalization factor in Section 9.

218

Selected electrons are further corrected by the reconstruction efficiency scale factor provided
by the EGamma POG [13], and mini-isolation scale factor from the SUSY group [12].

229

Isolated track veto

To further suppress the lost lepton backgrounds, we reject events that have one or more isolated tracks. The track isolation is calculated from charged PF candidates consistent with the
reconstructed primary vertex (|dz (PV)| < 0.1 cm and |dxy (PV)| < 0.2 cm).
The requirements are different for muon, electron and charged hadron tracks. For both electron
and muon tracks, the isolated track requirements are: pT > 5 GeV, |η | <2.5 and relative isolation less than 0.2, which is defined as the ratio of the pT sum of charged hadron within the ∆R
of 0.3 and the isolated track pT . For charged hadron tracks, the pT requirement is raised to be at
least 10 GeV and the relative isolation value to be less than 0.1. To retain more signal, and thus
improve signal-to-background event discrimination, events with one isolated track, as defined
above, are only rejected if they satisfy

4.4

Tau veto

To further reduce the lost lepton background, we veto events with an additional hadronic tau
in the event. On top of the isolated track veto, we further veto tau lepton candidates passing
tau ID from the Tau POG, in order to suppresses tau decays (mainly three-prong tau leptons)
not already vetoed by the isolated track veto. An event is vetoed if one or more hadronic tau
leptons are reconstructed, meeting the following criteria:

4.5

Photons

Estimation of the Z → νν̄ background makes use of a variety of control samples, including a
γ+jets sample. For the identification of photons in this sample we apply the medium working
point of the EGamma POG photon identification criteria. For 2016, we use Spring16-V2p2 ID,
while 2017 v2 ID is used for 2017/2018 ntuple. We select photons with pT > 220 GeV and
|η | < 2.5, excluding 1.4442 < |η | < 1.5660, which corresponds to the detector gap in ECAL.
The high pT requirement imposed on the selected photons is driven by the single photon trigger
threshold.

252

The selected photons are corrected by the pixel seed electron veto scale factor, and corresponding ID scale factor of each era from the EGamma POG [14].

Jets

The jets selected for use in this analysis are Particle Flow (PF) jets reconstructed with the anti-kT
algorithm [15] using a distance parameter of 0.4. The charged hadron subtraction procedure is
used to correct for pileup effects [16].
Jet energy corrections supplied by the JetMET POG are applied [17]. The L1FastJet, L2Relative,
and L3Absolute corrections are applied to data and simulation. The L2L3Residual corrections are
applied to the data.

270

b-tagging

B-tagged jets are identified using the DeepCSV tagger, which is a new algorithm based on the
same set of observables used by the previous CSVv2 b-tagger, with a simple extension to use
more charged particle tracks. This algorithm is based on a deep neural network training, with
four hidden layers (i.e. six layers altogether) of a width of 100 nodes each.
The medium working point recommended by the B-tag POG is used to tag b-jets for this analysis. We select b-tagged jets with pT > 20 GeV and |η | < 2.4.

275

As discussed in Section 5, we also utilize jets originating from initial state radiation (ISR) in
order to enhance top squark signal events which small mass splitting between t̃ and χe01 . The
ISR jet may undergo QCD radiation and results in two or more collinear jets, so we use large-R
jets of R = 0.8 reconstructed with the anti-kT algorithm, and we require that candidate ISR jets
to fail the loose b-tag working point.

276

The DeepCSV b-tagging working points are [7]:

271

• 2016: 0.6321 (Medium), 0.2217 (Loose)
• 2017: 0.4941 (Medium), 0.1522 (Loose)
• 2018: 0.4184 (Medium), 0.1241 (Loose)

283

The b-tagging efficiency depends on event kinematics. Therefore, we first compute the tagging
efficiencies for each flavor as a function of the jet pT and jet η within each simulated sample.
Then we obtain the b-tagging efficiency correction weight from the provided SF values from
the BTV POG.

Soft b-tagging

293

This search also targets models that produce very soft bottom or charm quarks. A large fraction
of such signal events contain b quarks with pT below the 20 GeV jet-pT threshold which may
fail to be reconstructed as jets or become b-tagged. Identification of these soft quarks improves
our ability to separate potential signal events from the SM background. We therefore aim to
identify b/c quarks based on the presence of a secondary vertex (SV) reconstructed using the
Inclusive Vertex Finder (IVF) algorithm. Additional requirements on SV observables are applied to suppress the background originating from light quarks. These selected SV may be
referred to as soft b tags and are constructed to be orthogonal to the jets and b-tagged jets used
in this analysis.

294

The requirements on each SV to pass the soft b-tagging definition are:

285
286
287
288
289
290
291
292

295
296
297
298
299
300
301
302

• The distance in the transverse plane between the SV and the PV is less than 3 cm
• The significance of the distance, SIP3D, between the SV and the PV is greater than 4
−−−−−→
• The pointing angle, defined as cos((PV, SV), ~pSV ), is greater than 0.98, where ~pSV is
the total four-momentum of the tracks associated to the SV
• The number of tracks associated to the SV is greater or equal to 3
• The pT of the SV is less than 20 GeV
• The distance to any jet with pT > 20 GeV, ∆R(jet, ~pSV ), is greater than 0.4 to achieve
the orthogonality to the jets and b-tagged jets

Boosted top- and W-tagging

The decay products of boosted top quarks with pT > 400 GeV, or W bosons with pT > 200 GeV,
are expected to be contained within a ∆R radius of 0.8. In this analysis, we use jets clustered
with the anti-kT algorithm using a distance parameter (R) of 0.8 (large-R jets), i.e. twice the
value used for the standard jet collection, i.e. the DeepAK8 [3, 4] algorithm for tagging merged
top quarks and W bosons.
This algorithm will identify top quarks with large boost, where the decay products are merged
into a single jet (merged top quark decay). We chose the working points shown in Table 12,
and the soft-drop mass cuts of 60 < mSD < 105 GeV and mSD > 105 GeV are applied on W and
top quark candidates, respectively to ensure the two categories are orthogonal. Additionally,
we apply a cut on |η | < 2.0 the top and W candidates to reduce contributions from high η
fakes with little effect on the efficiency. As for top quark candidates, the efficiency in lepton
+ hadronic-top events is 40% and the mistagging rate is 5% in dileptonic tt̄ events while the
mistagging rate in QCD multijet events is measured to be 0.5% for the chosen working point.
The top/W identification SF used are derived by the JMAR group for the working points used
in this analysis and are documented in [24]. The misidentification SF can differ from selection
to selection and so these are derived specifically for this analysis. The misidentification SFs
are discussed more in Appendix C. In addition to the SF and uncertanties applied to fullsim,
an additional correction and set of uncertainties is derived to correct the fastsim signal to the
fullsim. These are derived by comparing the signal yields for those signal points where both
fullsim and fastsim were generated. For the top tagger the fastsim scale factors are derived in
[25] while for the W tagger their derivation is summarized in [26].
We elect not to apply an upper bound to the soft-drop mass of the top quark candidates because
this has an adverse effect on the efficiency of T1tttt signals. Fig. 3 (left) shows the mass for a
T1tttt signal point with a gluino mass of 2000 GeV and χ̃01 mass of 100 GeV broken down into
the number of hard generator partons (u, d, s, c, b, or gluon) matched to the AK8 jet within a
∆R < 0.6 cone. A small but significant tail of the spectrum extends to high mass due to the
dense environment of the T1tttt signals.
We also look at the efficiency as a function of the number of matched generator partons, Fig. 3
(right), and see a downward trend. We expect some effect in the efficiency as the jet contains
more energy from other gen particles; however in order to cover for any difference in modeling
for the AK8 jets with many matched partons we assign a 20% uncertainty to any boosted top
which can be matched to 4 or more gen partons. This uncertainty is primarily intended to
cover any differences in the boosted top scale factors from limited statistics in the data for
tops with 4 or more matched partons and is derived from the change in efficiency seen in
simulation between 3 and 4 matched partons. This will primarily effect signal where there is a
much higher chance to have more matched generator partons, where as this is relatively rare
in standard model processes. This additional uncertainty is not applied to samples dominated

Resolved top-tagging

The analysis is currently using DeepResolved algorithm for tagging the resolved top quarks [5,
6], i.e. hadronically-decaying top quarks with moderate pT boost whose decay products result
in three separate jets. The DeepResolved algorithm identifies top quarks the decay products of
which form three anti-kT jets of distance parameter 0.4. The three jets (pT > 40, 30, 20 GeV) of
each candidate must have an invariant mass between 100 and 250 GeV, no more than one of the
jets can be identified as a b-tagged jet, and the three jets must all lie within a cone of ∆R < 3.14
of the trijet centroid.
A neural network is used to distinguish trijet combinations which match to a top quark versus
those which do not. The network uses high-level information such as the invariant mass of
the trijet system and of the individual dijet pairs, as well as kinematic information from each
jet. This includes its Lorentz vector, DeepCSV heavy-flavor discriminator values, jet shape
variables, and detector level particle multiplicity and energy fraction variables. The network is
trained using both tt̄ and QCD simulation, and data as training inputs. The simulation is used
to define the examples of signal and background. The signal is defined as any trijet passing
the preselection requirements, where each jet is matched to a generator level daughter of a
top quark within a cone of ∆R < 0.4 and the overall trijet system is matched to the generator
level top quark within a cone of ∆R < 0.6. The background category is defined as any trijet
combination that is not categorized as signal. This includes trijet combinations for which some,
but not all, of the jets match top decay products. The data is included in the training to inhibit
the network from learning features of the MC which are not present in data. This is achieved
through a technique called domain adaption via gradient reversal [27]. With this method, an

4. Object and event selection

369
370
371
372
373
374
375
376
377
378
379
380
381
382
383
384
385
386
387
388
389
390

19

additional output is added to the neural network to distinguishing between trijet candidates
from QCD simulation and a QCD-enriched data sample. The main network is then restricted
to minimize its ability to discriminate simulation from data. This yields a network with good
separation between signal and background while minimizing over-fitting on features that exist
only in simulation.
Before the final selection of trijets as top quarks can be made, any trijet candidates that may
share the jets with another candidate must be removed. This includes sharing a jet with another
trijet candidate, or with another AK8 top or W. The overlap with AK8 top and W is removed
by removing any AK4 jet from consideration if it is matched to an AK8 subjet within a cone
of ∆R < 0.4. In the rare case where a deepAK8 top or W has less than 2 subjets, AK4 jets are
removed if they overlap with the AK8 jet anywhere in its cone of ∆R < 0.8. The cross cleaning
between different trijet candidates is achieved by always favoring a candidate with a higher top
discriminator value as determined by the neural network. The final list of reconstructed top
quark candidates is then found by placing a requirement on the neural network discriminator.
An event has a resolved top quark tag if at least one top quark candidate has a discriminator
value above a threshold (of 0.92). Furthermore, the requirements that the trijet system has
|η | < 2.0 and that no more than one of the three jets be tagged as a b jet by the medium
working point of the deepCSV algorithm are used to reduce fakes. The working point chosen
corresponding to an efficiency to select a hadronically-decaying top quark with the resolved
tagger is up to 45% and the mistagging rate is 10% for dileptonic tt̄ events. The fakerate in
QCD multijet events is found to be 2% for the selected working point of the neural network
discriminator.

392

A more detailed discussion of the DeepResolved algorithm, along with a discussion of performance and data vs MC scale factors can be found in [5, 6].

ISR-tagging

Initial-state radiation (ISR) may be clustered into a large-R jet clustered with a distance parameter of 0.8. We use larger radius jets to be sensitive to ISR with gluon splitting. The ISR jet is
identified as being the hardest of the large-R jets with pT > 200 GeV and |η | < 2.4 in events
that do not contain boosted top or W candidates from the DeepAK8 tagger or resolved top candidates from the resolved top tagger. The ISR jet and its sub-jets are required
to fail the loose

miss
b-tagging working point. Finally, the ISR jet must satisfy ∆φ ~pT , jISR > 2.

439

HEM 15/16 failure

On June 30th 2018, two sectors of HE (HCAL endcap) become unresponsive and eventually unoperational. These two HE modules (a ∼40 degree sector) correspond to the region of −3.0 <
η < −1.4, −1.57 < φ < −0.87 and affect lepton, photon, jet and pmiss
reconstruction in that
T
region.
The simulation samples produced assume a well-conditioned detector. Thus we are applying
an event veto (HEMVeto) if there is any AK4 jet with pT > 30 GeV in the affected region (−3.2 <
η < −1.2, −1.77 < φ < −0.67). This wider η, φ window is used for AK4 jets which have a
radius of ∆R = 0.4, and it is the same η-φ window used for the SUSY RA2/b analysis [32]. The
HEM veto is applied for affected 2018 data starting from Run 319077 and the corresponding
Monte Carlo events. The overall effect on signal yield is small and does not affect the search
sensitivity.
In addition, we make use of lepton and photon control regions in order to predict some of our
backgrounds. The failure of two sectors of HE does not have much impact on muon identification, but there are non-negligible effects on electron and photon identification. The electron
and photon identification depends on the amount of energy measured by ECAL and HCAL.
We expect electromagnetic radiation to deposit a large portion of energy in ECAL and little or
no energy in HCAL. The failed HE modules effectively report zero energy measured in HCAL
for their sectors. Thus the portion of the detector with failed HE sectors measures an excess in
electrons and photons from misidentified hadronic jets.
For electrons and photons, we define an event veto as follows. Events are vetoed if there is an
electron with pT > 20 GeV or a photon with pT > 220 GeV in the region −3.0 < η < −1.4,
−1.57 < φ < −0.87.

Search strategy
Trigger

We use MET triggers as our main search triggers. The unprescaled MET trigger thresholds were
increased over time due to increasing instantaneous luminosity and consequently increasing
trigger rates. The triggers we use are:

•
•
•
•

HLT PFMETX PFMHTX IDTight (X=100,110,120,130,140)
HLT PFMETNoMuX PFMHTNoMuX IDTight (X=100,110,120,130,140)
HLT PFMETX PFMHTX IDTight PFHT60 (X=100,110,120,130,140)
HLT PFMETNoMuX PFMHTNoMuX IDTight PFHT60 (X=100,110,120,130,140)

456

The most inclusive triggers are PFMET100 PFMHT100 IDTight and
PFMETNoMu100 PFMHTNoMu100 IDTight. The former requires PFMET, which is the pmiss
T
calculated as the magnitude of the negative vector sum of the pT of all PF particles, to be greater
than 100 GeV and PFMHT, which is the HTmiss calculated with negative scalar sum of the pT of
all tight ID jets, to be greater than 100 GeV. The latter has a similar requirement, except that
muons are removed from PF particles. The other triggers, either with higher pT requirement,
or additional PFHT60 requirement, are backup triggers, which can provide efficiency in case
the most inclusive one is prescaled.

457

In addition we use electron, muon, and photon triggers for Z invisible background estimation.

458

We need to know efficiency of triggers we use. We use reference trigger method to measure
trigger efficiency.

Baseline event selection

Following the trigger requirements, a loose pre-selection, which we will refer to as the baseline
selection, that places additional criteria on jets and pmiss
is used to eliminate a large fraction

of background events. The baseline selection is defined by the following and summarized in

• Events pass the recommended MET filters as detailed in Section 4.11.

The vetoes on identified leptons and isolated tracks are applied in order to reduce the lostlepton background and to maintain orthogonality with top squark searches in leptonic events.
In addition to the baseline selection, two separate sets of additional selections are applied to
increase sensitivities for compressed signals with small mass differences between t̃ and χe0 (low∆m selection) and large mass differences (high-∆m selection). The low ∆m baseline selection
includes the following additional requirements to the baseline selection:

• Nt = 0, NW = 0, Nres = 0, where Nt and NW are the numbers of merged tops and

quarks (see Section 4.9 for details).

6

Backgrounds from top and W decays

The background from tt̄, W + jets and single-top events which are not removed by the explicit
lepton veto and isolated track veto cuts discussed in section 5.2 are the largest background
in the analysis. It can contain either a hadronically decaying tau or light leptons (electrons
or muons) which are not isolated, not identified/reconstructed or are out of the acceptance
region. These “lost lepton” backgrounds are estimated using the “translation factor method”
(described in this section).
The contribution from the tt̄ and W +jets processes arises from leptonic decays of the W boson,
where the charged lepton is outside the kinematic acceptance of CMS or evades identificaiton
by the dedicated lepton vetoes. Large pmiss
can be generated by the associated neutrino and
T
the lepton that is not reconstructed, allowing such events to enter the search regions. This
background is collectively referred to as the ”Lost Lepton” (LL) background. Contributions
arising from single top and tt̄W processes also enter into this category, but with much smaller
importance. We also include the tt̄Z and rare processes such as, VV, VVV, tt̄H, and tt̄γ, where
we include the requirement of at least one generator level charged lepton in the event. This
allows for them to be orthogonal to the prediction of the tt̄Z and rare background described in
Section 9.

649

Studies in simulation indicate that the event kinematics for different lepton flavors are similar
enough to allow us to estimate them collectively from a single control sample in data that has
event characteristics similar to those of the search sample. Because of this, we use the singlelepton control sample to estimate the LL background, using the method described in detail in
Ref [1]. The single-lepton sample consists of events that have one lepton satisfying the leptonveto criteria. In order to suppress potential signal contamination, we require mT (l, pmiss
T ) <
100 GeV. The requirement of low mT (l, pmiss
)
also
ensures
orthogonality
to
the
search
regions
T
used in direct top squark production in the single-lepton or double-lepton final state, making
it possible to statistically combine the results of the two searches. The selection applied to
the single-lepton control sample follows the same selection on the search variables as in the
zero-lepton selection with the exception of classification according to the number of top and
W-tagged candidates.

Transfer factor method

The LL estimation in each search region is based upon the event count in data in the corresponding control region in the single-lepton sample. The count is extrapolated to the search
region to obtain a prediction by means of a transfer factor obtained from simulation as follows:
LL
Npred
= TFLL · Ndata (1l ).

654
655
656
657

This allows us to have the same selection for the single-lepton control sample and the zerolepton sample. The only exception is the number of top and W-tagged candidates. The LL
estimation is dependent on the yield of data in the corresponding CR and the TF calculated by
the single-lepton sample. The transfer factor is defined as,
TFLL =

count in the corresponding SR.

The transfer factors in the high ∆m region actually account for two levels of extrapolation.
The CR for the high ∆m is loose such that, there is no binning in tops or W tagging. We then
extrapolate to the SR with the inclusion of the top and W tags, along with scale factors, to
estimate the LL background in the region:

669

The main motivation behind this approach is to increase the statistical precision of the background estimation, and is the same as used in Ref. [1]. The performance of the t and W taggers
has been studied in data and MC samples and a reasonably good agreement is observed allowing us to proceed with this approach. Data-to-MC scale factors are extracted and applied
to MC to account for residual differences of the tagging performance in data. Detailed studies
comparing the performance of the t and W taggers in data and MC can be found in Refs. [4–6].

Combining all Run 2 eras

For this full Run 2 analysis, we are interested in the possibility in combining the yields of each
era into one estimation. This is initially done by looking at the pmiss
distributions in each era,
T
which are shown in Figs. 11 and 12 for the low ∆m and high ∆m inclusive regions, which are
not including the bin specific cuts in each region. The decreasing trend in data/MC versus
pmiss
shown in Fig. 11, observed in all eras, has been seen also in earlier analyses in the regions
T
dominated by W+jets (e.g. [8] Fig. 14, [32] Fig. 11). The trend is not an issue for the data-driven
background estimate using TF as it is performed in bins of pmiss
as well as all other search
T
variables except for the number of top and W-tagged candidates. The data-MC agreement
tends to be better in the tt̄-dominated high ∆m region as shown in Fig. 12, and the trend in the
comparison in the low and high ∆m regions is accounted for by the transfer factor method.
Since the LL estimation is done with the transfer factor method, a good confirmation would
be the comparison of the transfer factor in each SR for each era, which is discussed below and
shown in Figs. 14 and 13. As shown in Fig. 14, the transfer factor, TFLL see Eq. 3, from different
eras show reasonable agreement, resulting in the TFera /TFAll around 1; however there are tails
on both high and low side. The TFAll is the combined yield of the years which is then used
CR-SR , shown in Fig. 13, where
to calculate the TFLL . The tails are much reduced in the TFLL
SR-extrap
CR-SR has smaller MC statistical uncertainties than the total
TFLL
is factored out, and TFLL
TFLL . It appears that there is no significant systematic data era dependences in TFLL . Now that
we have confirmed that the LL background is valid to be combined for all the eras, since the
TFLL comparisons are near 1 and the inclusive regions show similar distributions.

6.3

Single lepton control region

The control regions utilized to predict the LL background are displayed in Figs. 15 to 21. Figures 15 to 16 display the control regions specific to the low ∆m selection, where the regions
are binned following the search region definition. Figures 17 to 21 display the control regions
dedicated for the high ∆m selection. Due to the nature of the background estimation method
applied in the high ∆m search, control regions are utilized for the prediction of multiple search
regions.

Extrapolation of the control region

The extrapolation of the lost lepton CR, described in Eq. (4), has been studied extensively. The
M AD G RAPH tt̄ samples used in this analysis give disgreement with 1-lepton CR data in terms
of Nt , NW , and Nres distributions, and it was considered that the disagreement arises from
the top pT spectrum mis-modeling. After studying various possible corrections/reweighting
options basedon e.g. leading AK8 jet pT and the 4-vector sum the lepton, b-quark, and pmiss
T
pT (l, b, pmiss
T ) , it was determined that a correction based on the generator level top pT , designed for POWHEG, along with a correction from the POWHEG tt̄ MC to the M AD G RAPH tt̄ MC
is most optimal for this analysis [33]. More details can be found in Ref. [33] and Appendix D.
This method gives a better agreement since it uses unfolded top quark pT spectra and apply
reweighting at the generator level, instead of using reconstructed kinematics. Some concerns
such as the signal contamination in the lost lepton CR, which is a similar region in which the
correction was derived, has been shown to be about 1% and has little effect. In Fig. 22, we
compare the prediction of the lost lepton background with and without the extrapolation in
top and W multiplicities in the high ∆m control regions. The prediction and uncertainties, both
statistical and systematic, using the data driven estimation with extrapolation is more stable

Systematic uncertainties
Lepton vetoes

Since the Lost Lepton background depends on lepton identification, the systematics for identifying an electron or muon are of interest for our search regions and control regions. The control
region of the LL is defined as having a single muon or electron. We must account for the acceptance and scale factor corrections for each lepton. The approximate value for the uncertainties
is 1–3% and < 1% uncertainty for electrons and muons, respectively depending on the control
region. It is also necessary to account for the effect of the lepton data/MC scale factor and its
uncertainties on event yields with the lepton veto. This is evaluated as shown in Eq. 5:
yield

Veto Efficiency = 1 +

Nl

− NlSF
NSR

(5)

yield

735

where Nl
and NlSF are the yields of simulation in the search region with a requirement of
Nl ≥ 1 without and with the lepton scale factor and NSR is the yield in the search region with
the lepton veto. The impact has an approximate value of 2–7%, 1%, and 2–7% uncertainty for
electrons, muons, and taus, respectively depending on the search region.

736

6.5.2

732
733
734

Merged top/W and resolved top

746

In search regions with requirements of top/W tagging, the tt̄ and W+jets background are the
dominant SM backgrounds. The systematics regarding the identification of resolved tops or
merged top/W are studied in this region. The impact of the resolved top scale factor is evaluated using the resolved tagger. Systematics for the merged top/W tagging is calculated to
be 8–14% and 6–18% for top and W tags, respectively. The resolved top tagging systematic is
calculated to be between 5–22% depending on the search region. The largest uncertainties arise
from high ∆m search bins which require different constraints on these objects. The merged
tops have an additional 20% uncertainty added when the gen-level partons within the top are
greater than 4. This correstponds to a 3–16% and 1–7% uncertainty for resolved tops with less
than 4 gen-level partons and greater than 4, respectively depending on the search region.

747

6.5.3 tt̄ and W + jets cross-section

737
738
739
740
741
742
743
744
745

752

The uncertainties on tt̄ and W + jets cross-section are taken into account to capture the mixture
of tt̄ and W + jets in different search bins. The uncertainties of 10% and 20% are assinged
to W + jets and tt̄, respectively, based on the differential cross section measurements of W +
jets [34] and tt̄ [35]. These contribute to give an uncertainty between 4–18% depending on the
search bin.

762

Top pT correction/reweighting
gen

Using the method described in Refs. [36, 37], we correct the top pT distribution up to 800 GeV
gen
(and the reweighting factor at pT = 800 GeV is used for higher pT values). The correction
(reweighting factor) is nominally about 0.8 for events passing our search selection and leads
to better agreement between the predictions with and without using the extrapolation method.
The systematic uncertainties are separated into the two parts of the correction. For the POWHEG
to M AD G RAPH correction, the differences between the correction ”on/off” is considered for
gen
uncertainties, while for the top pT reweighting the uncertainty on the fit from Refs. [36, 37] is
gen
considered. These contribute to about between 3–5% and 10–20% uncertainty for the top pT
and POWHEG/M AD G RAPH reweighting methods, respectively.

7

Backgrounds from neutrinos in Z decays

One of the important SM backgrounds for our top squark search is events containing a Z boson
that decays to neutrinos. The branching fraction of Z to neutrinos is 0.20000 ± 0.00055 [38], and
there are a significant number of Z to neutrino events in many of our search bins. We will refer
to this background as the “Z invisible” background. The CMS detector cannot measure tracks
or energy deposits from neutrinos. Thus for Z invisible events, we do not explicitly measure
the momenta of the neutrinos from the Z boson. However, we do measure missing transverse
momentum pmiss
which is the negative of the transverse component of the sum of the momenta
T
of all particles in an event. The Z momentum will typically have a significant contribution to
the value of pmiss
when the Z decays to neutrinos.
T

773

7.1

Method

774

The Z invisible background estimation is performed based on:

Z →νν̄

Z →νν̄

Npred = RZ · Sγ · NMC ,
775
776
777

Z →νν̄

(6)
Z →νν̄

where Npred is the predicted number of Z invisible events. The Z → νν̄ MC yields, NMC ,
are corrected with factors obtained in two control
  regions. We use a dilepton control region
to extract a normalization correction factor

RZ

as a function of the number of b jets ( Nb )

784

and number of soft b jets ( NSV ) that accounts for differences between data and simulation
in the cross-section of the Z → νν̄ process. The RZ correction is obtained from Z → e+ e− and Z → µ+ µ− -enriched control regions.
In addition, we use a single photon control region

to obtain a shape correction factor Sγ that accounts for shape differences between data and
simulation for the Z → νν̄ process. This factor is obtained from a γ+jets control region based
on the similarity between Z+jets and γ+jets processes when the Z and γ both have transverse
momentum significantly higher than the Z mass.

Normalization factor

We use a dilepton control region to calculate a normalization factor. The control region is split
into a dielectron control region and a dimuon control region. In both of these regions, we select
two oppositely charged same flavor leptons. The electron and muon pT cuts are chosen based
on the trigger efficiencies discussed in Section 5.1. We require pT > 40 GeV for the leading
electron and pT > 20 GeV for the subleading electron. We require pT > 50 GeV for the leading
muon and pT > 20 GeV for the subleading muon. In addition, we select electrons with |η | < 2.5
and muons with |η | < 2.4. We use medium ID for both electrons and muons as discussed in
Sections 4.1 and 4.2. Furthermore, in order to reduce tt̄ contamination, we place a dilepton pT
cut of pT > 200 GeV. Figure 24 shows the dilepton pT distribution without this cut applied.
Jets that are matched to leptons are removed from the jet collection for calculating all jet dependent variables. For the purpose of this jet cleaning, AK4 jets with ∆R < 0.2 with a lepton
are considered matched, and AK8 jets with ∆R < 0.4 with a lepton are considered matched.
All jet dependent variables are then recalculated after lepton cleaning, including HT , ∆φ, pbT ,
mbT , and the number of b-jets, soft b-jets, merged tops, resolved tops, and Ws. The two lepton
four-vectors are added to reconstruct the Z four-vector. However, we treat the reconstructed Z
as pmiss
to mimic the Z decay to neutrinos. Thus we add the Z four-vector to the ~pTmiss and take
T
the transverse component, which is referred to as modified pmiss
T .

To account for potential effects related to heavy flavor production, RZ and R T are measured
independently for different Nb and NSV requirements of low ∆m and high ∆m regions as shown
in Table 23. Figs. 25, 26 and 27 show example Mll distributions from which the RZ and R T
factors are extracted. Some search bins have the requirement Nb ≥ 3. However, there are not
enough events in the dilepton control region passing Nb ≥ 3 to provide a precise normalization
value. For the search bins with Nb ≥ 3, we the value of RZ obtained in the Nb ≥ 2 region which
has an adequate number of events.
Furthermore, RZ and R T are calculated independently in the dielectron and dimuon channels.
These two channels are statistically independent as for the dielectron selelction we apply a
muon veto, and for the dimuon selection we apply an electron veto. The values of RZ and R T
are obtained by inverting the matrix in Eq. 7. The statistical uncertainties for RZ and R T are derived by propagating the statistical uncertainties in MC and data throughout the calculation in
Eq. 7. The RZ value used for the Z invisible prediction is the weighted average of the dielectron

7.3

Shape factor

We use a photon control region to calculate a shape factor. The photon pT cut is based on the
photon trigger efficiency discussed in Section 5.1. Photons are required to have pT > 220 GeV
and |η | < 1.4442 or 1.5660 < |η | < 2.5. These eta cuts reject photons that are in the gap
between the ECAL barrel and the ECAL endcap. We use a medium photon id as discussed in
Section 4.5. Jets that are matched to the photon are removed from the jet collection. Similarly
to the lepton jet cleaning, AK4 jets with ∆R < 0.2 with a photon are considered matched, and
AK8 jets with ∆R < 0.4 with a photon are considered matched. However, we treat the photon
miss and take the transverse component
as pmiss
T . Thus we add the photon four-vector to the pT
miss(γ)

to give the modified pT
. We also place a cut on the intrinsic pmiss
(the original pmiss
in the
T
T
miss
event) of pT < 250 GeV to make the photon control region orthogonal to the search region.
The reco photon which we select in the single photon control region can come from various
sources. We define the following photon categories to describe different processes which can
produce reco photons passing our selection. For gen-reco photon matching, the requirements
gen
are ∆R (gen photon, reco photon) < 0.1 and 0.5 < preco
< 2.0. The shorthand ∆Rγp is
T /pT
used to represent ∆R (photon, parton).

• Direct
• gen matched to prompt status 1 gen photon
• ∆Rγp > 0.4 for all prompt status 23 gen partons
• Fragmentation
• gen matched to prompt status 1 gen photon
• ∆Rγp < 0.4 for at least one prompt status 23 gen parton
• Nonprompt

• gen matched to nonprompt status 1 gen photon
• Fake

Both γ + jets and QCD MC are considered for the photon control region. For the sake of efficient
computing resource usage, the M AD G RAPH γ + jets sample has the requirement ∆Rγp > 0.4
on prompt generator level photons compared to all generator level hard partons. The QCD MC
also has events with prompt-photons in this phase space. In order to avoid double counting
in this phase space, we veto QCD events which have at least one generator level photon that
is prompt, matched to the selected reco photon passing the photon selection, and passes the
∆Rγp > 0.4 requirement. This QCD event veto is referred to as the “QCD overlap cut.” For
the QCD overlap cut, generated photons are selected with PDG ID 22, PYTHIA status 1 (stable),
and status flag 1 (prompt), and generated partons are selected with PDG ±1–6 (quarks), 9 or
21 (gluons), PYTHIA status 23 (outgoing from hardest subprocess), and status flag 1 (prompt).
miss(γ)

Data and simulation in the photon control region are shown as a function of pT
(Fig. 28)
and HT (Fig. 29) with QCD separated into the various photon categories defined above. We can
measure the effect of potential mismodeling of fragmentation, nonprompt, and fake photons.
To do this, each of these contributions is independently varied by ±50% to represent a large
amount of mismodeling. Then the shape factor is recalculated and compared to the nominal
shape factor. The results are shown in Figs. 30 and 31. The photon control region is dominated
by γ + jets events, and the fragmentation, nonprompt, and fake photons from QCD have only a
small contribution to the shape factor. When the different QCD photon categories are varied by
±50%, the change in the shape factor is small (1–5%). Therefore no dedicated correction factors
or additional uncertainties are required regarding different photon categories. For the shape
factor used for the Z → νν̄ prediction, QCD is not separated into different photon categories.

Combining eras

The CMS Run 2 dataset we use for this analysis come from three years of data taking, i.e. 2016,
2017, and 2018. For the Z invisible background prediction, we examine the normalization and
shape factors in different eras to determine if there are any important differences that need to
be accounted for. The era (run period) dependence of measured RZ is shown in Figs. 34–35.

The RZ factor is generally stable over different run periods, so we use the combined RZ factor
for the full Run 2 data period. For some regions where the measurement has small statistical
uncertainties (e.g. low ∆m, Nb = 0, and NSV = 0), variations of RZ are larger than statistical
fluctuations, and we assign additional systematic uncertainties to cover the variations.
The era (run period) dependence of measured Sγ distributions is shown in Figs. 36–37. The
distributions are generally stable over different run periods, so we use a combined set of Sγ
shape correction factors for the full Run 2 data period.

7.5

Systematic uncertainties

Because the central estimate of the Z → νν̄ method comes from the Z → νν̄ MC, many of
the systematic uncertainties applied to the Z → νν̄ background are shared with ttZ/Rare
background and signal estimates and are discussed in detail in Sections 9 and 10. These
“standard” MC uncertainties come from the following sources: JES, pmiss
unclustered energy,
T
lepton/photon/b-jet scale factors, trigger efficiency estimations, pileup, top/W tagger scale
factors, PDFs, and MC scale and renormalization. All uncertainties are applied to the Z → νν̄
MC as well as the background MC used in the photon control region. There are two additional
sources of uncertainty which are specific to the Z → νν̄ background estimate.
The first additional the systematic uncertainty is applied to the normalization factor, RZ , to
cover differences seen between different run eras which are not covered by the statistical uncertainties of the factor as seen in Figs. 34–35. The central value of RZ is derived by summing
the three eras discussed in Sec. 7.4. The additional uncertainty is then estimated by taking the
S = χ2 /NDOF between the central value
√ and the three individual eras and scaling the statistical uncertainty on the central value by S. This method is based on the approach described in
Sec. 5.2.2 of Ref. [38].
The second Z → νν̄ specific systematic is designed to cover any residual differences between
the modeling of Z + jets events with γ + jets events. This systematic is derived by looking at
the double ratio between data and MC in the dilepton control region and photon control region
as a function of pmiss
T , shown in Fig. 38. In order to make the best use of the low statistics in
the dilepton control region, we make the comparison inclusive inside the low ∆m and high ∆m
selections. Any deviation from unity is considered to be due to modeling differences between
the Z + jets and γ + jets processes in the control regions. The larger of either the absolute
deviation of the ratio from unity or the statistical uncertainty is then taken as the systematic
uncertainty as a function of the modified pmiss
of the events.
T

Z →νν̄
(Fig. 40). The normalization factor RZ , shape factor Sγ , number of Z → νν̄ MC events NMC ,
Z →νν̄

and the background prediction Npred including statistical uncertainties are provided for each
search bin in Tables 24, 25, 26, and 27.

8. Backgrounds from QCD multijets

939

8

77

Backgrounds from QCD multijets

945

The QCD multijet background originates from mismeasurement of the energy of one or more
jets in a SM multijet event. When that happens, large amounts of spurious pmiss
can be present
T
in the reconstructed event, which potentially mimics an all-hadronic SUSY final state that
passes the search selection. The probability to have such an event, where there are fake b jets
and tops, is very low, but the high QCD cross section makes them very numerous and therefore
their contribution to the signal regions must be estimated.

General approach

Simulation generally predicts negligible levels of contamination in the various search regions.
However, the QCD multijet simulation has limited statistics and there are uncertainties related
to the description of physics in the simulation, particularly for the rare scenarios that would
lead to a multijet event passing all of the final search region selection criteria. For these reasons, it is necessary to use a partially data-driven QCD background estimation. We follow an
approach similar to those described for other SM backgrounds: first using a QCD-enhanced
region to validate the simulation, then extrapolating the event count in the control region to a
prediction in the search region.
In QCD events, pmiss
is generated through either jet pT mis-measurement or semi-leptonic
T
heavy flavor decay. For the purposes of this section both sources of pmiss
will be generally
T
miss
referred to as mis-measurement. This causes pT to be nearly collinear with one of the jets in
the event, which in turn motivates a veto on such events in the baseline selection described in
Section 5.2. The veto is a requirement that the azimuthal separation ∆φ between pmiss
and jets
T
1, 2, and 3 is greater than 0.5, 0.15, and 0.15, respectively. Inverting this veto yields a fairly pure
sample of QCD multijet events with fake pmiss
T .
We define the QCD control region by not only inverting the veto, but, to further improve the
purity, we require at least one of the three leading jets to lie within 0.1 of the pmiss
in the azT
imuthal angle φ (referred to as ∆φ123 < 0.1). The QCD control region definition otherwise
uses the baseline selection described in Section 5.2, including the “low-∆m selection” and the
“high-∆m selection” subregions (with the exception, of course, of the additional ∆φ cut in the
high-∆m selection, which is dropped for the high-∆m QCD control subregion).
b
b
ISR
The QCD control region is binned in pmiss
T , HT , the number of jets, mT , pT , pT , the number of
b-tagged jets, and the number of soft-b-tagged jets, with the bin numbers and ranges matching the search bins described in Section 5.3. Note that the control region is not binned in the
numbers of merged tops, merged Ws, or resolved tops. This allows us to maintain adequate
statistics in each bin of the control region.

To compute the event yields from simulation, we weight the simulated events for the luminosity, theoretical cross section, and number of simulated events in the usual fashion. We further
weight the simulated events to account for the efficiency of the pmiss
trigger as well as for difT
ferences between the simulation and reality in b tagging, soft-b tagging, merged-top tagging,
merged-W tagging, resolved-top tagging, and event pileup. In the 2016 simulation of tt̄, an additional weight is applied to correct the amount of initial-state radiation. In the 2016 and 2017
simulation, an additional weight is applied to account for unsimulated prefire effects. In the
simulation of tt̄ in all years, a weight is applied to correct simulated spectrum of the transverse
momenta of the top quark and top antiquark.
The yields of QCD multijet in the search bins are extrapolated from the corresponding bin(s)
in this QCD control region. The ratio of the search region QCD yield to the control region

78
984
985

QCD
QCD yield, TFQCD , is taken from simulation, and the QCD background yield, Npred
, may be
estimated as:



non-QCD
Npred
= TFQCD · Ndata − NMC
986

988

non-QCD
where Ndata is the number of data events in the QCD control region and NMC
is the number
of non-QCD MC events in the QCD control region. The ∆φ123 < 0.1 requirement is applied in
the control region as described above.

997

For the statistical analysis discussed in Section 11, this approach is incorporated into the final
fit by including a separate nuisance parameter for each search bin that allows the QCD yield
in the search bin and in the corresponding control-region bin to float while keeping the ratio
between regions fixed. In this way, the data (and non-QCD simulation) in the QCD control
region constrains the QCD multijet yield in the search region in a manner essentially equivalent
to the transfer-factor method described in the previous paragraph. This nuisance-parameter
method has the advantage that it is well-behaved even in regions of very low statistics, and
handling of statistical and systematic uncertainty is simpler than it would be when using the
transfer-factor method.

Smearing

The Monte Carlo simulation of QCD multijet production does not contain enough statistics
to produce a reliable estimate of the QCD multijet yield. This is due to the fact that large
mis-measurement is rather rare, so the overwhelming majority of the simulated events do not
contain sufficient pmiss
to enter our data set. In order to reduce the uncertainty due to the low
T
statistics of the simulation, we use a “smearing” procedure as also done in earlier analyses [8,
39].
Briefly, each simulated event is copied 100 times, with the reconstructed pT of each of the two
leading jets randomly sampled according to the expected jet response in each copy. This allows
a much larger fraction of the originally-simulated events to enter our data set via at least one of
the 100 copies, resulting in a dramatic improvement in the statistical uncertainty of our QCD
multijet yield prediction at the cost of some added complexity and bin-to-bin correlations in
the statistical uncertainty of the QCD multijet yield prediction.
To avoid bias caused by variations in the jet-energy response as a function of η and φ, the
smearing of each jet is only performed in a small window centered on the jet-energy response,
rjet ≡

1013
1014
1015

p T,reco
,
p T,gen

of the jet prior to smearing. This is sometimes called “local smearing”. Additionally, the jetenergy response distribution is parameterized as a function of generator-level jet pT and jet
flavor (b vs non-b).

1018

Each smeared copy is assigned a weight. The weights are calculated to make the weighted distribution of smeared events match the distribution of events before smearing. The derivation
of these weights follows.

1019

The joint distribution of the jet response before smearing, r, and the smeared jet response, r 0 , is

We assign a systematic uncertainty to cover this possible bias.
Other choices of weights may also be possible, especially if the sampling strategy is changed,
but these weight choices help to provide larger numbers of small-weight MC events in the tails
of the distribution, which ultimately improves the statistical power of the MC sample.
The above calculation describes a weight α(r ) β(r 0 ) for each smeared jet. The weight for each
event is simply the product of the per-jet weights for the two smeared jets.
In the QCD control region, the agreement between the smeared and non-smeared QCD samples
is quite good, but in the search region, some residual bias is present as seen in Figure 41. This
bias is covered by the systematic uncertainty mentioned above.

8.2.1

Bootstrapping

In order to estimate the statistical uncertainty in the smeared QCD sample, we use a bootstrapping method. For each pre-smeared event, a larger number of bootstrap weights is generated.
Each weight is a sample from a Poisson distribution with a mean of 1. These weights are then
stored with each of the 100 copies of the event after smearing. Note that this means that each
of the 100 smeared copies of each event has exactly the same set of bootstrap weights as all of
the other copies of that event, but that distinct events have distinct sets of bootstrap weights.
When making histograms, the “nominal” histogram is made using all of the smeared events
without any bootstrap weight applied. Then, a separate histogram is made using each bootstrap weight. The standard deviation of the resulting set of bootstrap histograms is then a good
approximation to the statistical uncertainty associated with the “nominal” histogram.

1061

Additionally, because some of the copies of a single pre-smeared event may fall into different
bins of a histogram, there are in general non-zero bin-to-bin correlations. These are estimated
by calculating the sample covariance of the bins in the collection of bootstrap histograms and
found to be negligible, so these correlations are ignored in the rest of the analysis.

1062

8.3

Jet resolution correction

The Monte Carlo simulation is known to underestimate the jet-energy resolution, that is, the
width of the jet-energy response function P(rjet ). The use of this response function in the smearing procedure exacerbates this problem, and so a correction is needed.
We derive a set of scale factors in the QCD control region, which are applied as event weights
when making histograms from the smeared simulated QCD events, to correct the jet-energy
response distribution. If rjet were known in the data, this would be a simple matter of taking the
ratio, in each of several rjet bins, of the data yield (less the simulated non-QCD backgrounds) to
the simulated QCD yield. Because we do not know rjet in the data (this would require knowing
the “true” jet pT ), we must construct a proxy variable using only reconstructed quantities that
approximates rjet . The proxy variable we use is
rpseudo,jet ≡

1073
1074
1075
1076

1078

1081

1083

1086
1087

p T,reco
,
p T,reco + pmiss
T

which is based on the assumption that pmiss
is closely aligned with a mis-measured jet. The
T
jet in each event that is used to calculate rjet and rpseudo,jet , which would normally be jet-level
quantities rather than event-level quantities, is the jet which falls closest to pmiss
in the azT
imuthal angle φ.
The rpseudo,jet distribution is divided into a large number of bins, and the spectrum in data is
compared to the spectrum in the simulated backgrounds, both QCD and non-QCD. Then, the
simulated QCD sample is divided into five bins in rjet , and a scale factor is derived for each
rjet bin by fitting to the data. The five QCD templates are shown with the data and non-QCD
background before and after fitting in Figure 42. The rjet bin boundaries are 0, 1/3, 1/2, 2/3,
5/6, and ∞. The resulting scale factors are given in Table 28.
These scale factors are used to weight the smeared, simulated QCD events in both the QCD
control region and the search region. Data-MC comparisons in the QCD control regions after
the rjet correction are shown in Fig. 43, and reasonable agreement is observed. The residual
data-MC disagreements should have smaller impacts on the QCD background estimations using TFQCD if differences are similar between the QCD control region and search region. The

Results

The resulting prediction for the QCD yield in the search bins is shown in Tables 29 through 32
and in Figure 45. The statistical and systematic uncertainties are listed for the predicted QCD
yield. The tables also contain the transfer factor along with its systematic uncertainty, and the
amount of data in the relevant bin in the QCD control region. Note that each bin in the QCD
control region may be used to extrapolate to multiple search bins. The result is that the tables
contain substantial repetition in the column giving the data rate in the QCD control region.
In Figure 45, the QCD prediction is shown with its uncertainty broken down into the components arising from the limited statistics of the data in the QCD control region, the limited
statistics of the Monte Carlo simulation, and all other sources of systematic uncertainty, which
are described below.
Additionally, Figure 46 shows the expected QCD yield in the validation bins, with the same
breakdown of the uncertainty.

Systematic uncertainties

1111

A number of sources of systematic uncertainty affect the QCD background estimation. In general, each source of systematic uncertainty may affect the QCD simulation in both the search
region and the QCD control region, the jet-response correction scale factors, and the simulation of non-QCD backgrounds in the QCD control region (which in turn affect the jet-response
correction scale factors).

1149

Simulation re-weighting systematics

Several of the sources of systematic uncertainty affect all of the simulations (both QCD and
non-QCD) simultaneously by changing the weight of each simulated event slightly, but do not
alter any simulated event other than the weight. These are

• the b-tag scale factor uncertainty (which is, averaged over the search bins, a 5 %
uncertainty on the predicted QCD yield),
• the soft-b-tag scale factor uncertainty (average 0.5 %),
• the parton-distribution function (PDF) uncertainty (average 8 %),
• the merged-top and merged-W scale factor uncertainties (average 2.5 % and 1 %,
respectively),
• the resolved-top scale factor uncertainty (average 5 %),
• and the pileup-weight uncertainty (average 4 %).
Additionally, the prefire-weight uncertainty (average 0.4 %) affects only the 2016 and 2017 simulations, and the initial-state-radiation (ISR) weight uncertainty (average 0.7 %) affects only the
tt̄ simulation in 2016.
In each of these cases, the event weight in question is varied first up and then down by one
standard deviation, and the entire QCD prediction procedure (including the re-derivation of
the jet-response correction scale factors) is repeated to produce a pair of QCD predictions (one
for the variation up by one standard deviation and one for the variation down by one standard
deviation).
The trigger efficiency uncertainty also affects only the weights of the simulated events, but it is
not derived solely from variations in the trigger efficiency up and down by one standard deviation. Because QCD multijet events do not contain real pmiss
T , we anticipate that the turn-on
curve of the pmiss
-based
trigger
is
different
than
for
events
with
real pmiss
T
T . We handle this by remeasuring the trigger efficiency in the QCD control region (which is dominated by QCD multijet events). This fake-pmiss
trigger efficiency is used to weight all simulated QCD events in both
T
the QCD control region and in the search region, while the default, real-pmiss
trigger efficiency
T
measured in the search region is used to weight all simulated non-QCD events in both the QCD
control region and in the search region. We conservatively estimate the uncertainty inherent
in this procedure by swapping our scheme: we re-weight the simulated non-QCD events in
the QCD control region with the fake-pmiss
trigger efficiency and re-weight the simulated QCD
T
events in the search region with the real-pmiss
trigger efficiency. We then run through the entire
T
QCD prediction procedure with these varied weights to produce an alternate QCD prediction
in the QCD control region and the search region. The difference between this alternate QCD
prediction and the nominal QCD prediction is then taken as the trigger-efficiency systematic
uncertainty and applied symmetrically on both sides of the nominal QCD prediction. The result is a uncertainty of about 12 % of the nominal QCD prediction when averaged over the
search bins.

8.5.2

Other systematic uncertainties

A few other sources of systematic uncertainty do not simply re-weight the simulated events.
The jet-energy-scale (JES) uncertainty, the unclustered-energy uncertainty, and some specialized uncertainties related to the QCD smearing procedure and jet-response correction fall into
this category and must be explained individually.
8.5.2.1 JES
The JES affects the reconstructed energy of every jet in each simulated event.
As a result, the event as a whole is changed. The merged-top, merged-W, b, soft b, and
resolved-top tags may change, as does pmiss
T , HT , etc. To estimate the eventual effects of JES
variation on the QCD prediction, the reconstructed energy of every jet in every simulated event
(both QCD and non-QCD) is shifted first up and then down by one standard deviation of the
JES. All of the variables used in the analysis are updated correspondingly. The QCD prediction
procedure is then repeated using all and only the simulated data sets with JES shifted up, and
again with all and only the simulated data sets with JES shifted down. The resulting variations
in the QCD prediction in both the search region and the QCD control region then represent the
effects of the JES uncertainty on the QCD prediction. The result is a roughly 18 % uncertainty
when averaged over the search bins.
8.5.2.2 Unclustered energy
Similarly, changes in the unclustered energy affect many of
the variables used in the analysis. So, for each simulated event (both QCD and non-QCD),
the unclustered energy is shifted, first up and then down by one standard deviation. All of
the affected variables are updated, and the QCD prediction procedure is repeated to estimate
the effects of the unclustered-energy uncertainty on the QCD prediction. This results in an
uncertainty of about 15 % averaged over search bins.
8.5.2.3 Smearing
We conservatively estimate any systematic uncertainties or biases associated with the smearing procedure by performing the entire QCD prediction procedure except
for smearing. Half of the difference between this “no-smearing” QCD prediction and the nominal QCD prediction is taken as the uncertainty induced by the smearing procedure, and is
applied symmetrically on both sides of the nominal QCD prediction. In order to maintain adequate statistics in the un-smeared QCD Monte Carlo, we integrate out all the search variables
except for pmiss
and HT when estimating this systematic. This produces a 12 % uncertainty
T
averaged over the search bins.
8.5.2.4 Residual mismodeling of pmiss
The simulation slightly overpredicts the rate of
T
events in the QCD control region with large pmiss
T . In general, this mismodeling is expected
to be present in roughly equal degree in both the QCD control region and the search region.
As a result, the mismodeling will be cancelled out in the extrapolation procedure. But, we
combine some pmiss
bins in the QCD control region when performing the extrapolation to the
T
search region in order to avoid having bins with zero events in data. Because of this, we are not
completely confident that the mismodeling is totally cancelled out. To conservatively account
for any residual mismodeling, we take half the difference between the data and Monte Carlo
in the QCD control region as a function of pmiss
as a systematic on the QCD Monte Carlo in the
T
QCD control region. This produces a 5 % uncertainty averaged over the search bins.
8.5.2.5 Jet-response correction
Similarly, we conservatively estimate any systematic
uncertainties or biases associated with the jet-response correction by performing the entire
QCD prediction procedure except for the jet-response correction. Half of the difference between this alternate QCD prediction and the nominal QCD prediction is taken as the uncertainty induced by the jet-response correction procedure, and is applied symmetrically on both

8. Backgrounds from QCD multijets

1195
1196
1197
1198
1199
1200

87

sides of the nominal QCD prediction. This gives us about a 12 % uncertainty when averaged
over the search bins.
Additionally, we propagate the statistical uncertainty on the jet-response scale factors themselves through to the final QCD prediction. This is driven primarily by the statistics of the data
in the QCD control region, and results in a small uncertainty, roughly 0.4 % on average among
the search bins.

9

Backgrounds from ttZ and other SM rare processes

Besides the dominant backgrounds discussed above, other SM backgrounds with small cross
sections were also considered and estimated for this analysis. These include the diboson (WW,
WZ, ZZ) processes, multiboson (WWW, WWZ, WZZ, ZZZ) processes, the associated production with a top quark pair (tt̄H, tt̄γ, tt̄W, and tt̄Z), and others (tWZ, WZγ, WWγ). Due to
requiring the presence of a reconstructed top candidate in high ∆m search bins, the processes
associated with top quarks are expected to have higher selection efficiency than the rest. In
particular, the tt̄Z process has an identical final state to some signals when the Z decays to
two neutrinos. The Feynman diagram of the dominant tt̄Z production mechanisms in protonproton collisions is shown in Fig. 47.

Figure 47: Dominant leading-order Feynman diagram for tt̄Z production at the LHC.

1216

The lost-lepton background uses a data driven method, which takes into account the leptonic
component of these backgrounds. The Z invisible background method corrected the Z invisible
MC to data. This leaves only the hadronic component of these rare backgrounds missing in the
analysis. To remove the overlap between these background predictions and the lost lepton
background prediction, a veto on generator-level leptons from hard processes is applied in the
final yield.

1235

ttZ Background

Much like the Z (→ νν)+jets background, tt̄Z is an irreducible background when Z decays
to νν and both top quarks decay hadronically. The tt̄Z cross section at 13 TeV is 0.7826 pb.
The predicted yield of tt̄Z events in the search bins is less than 10% of the total background.
Given the small cross section associated with this process, we rely on simulation to generate a
prediction, although this estimation is validated using real collider data. A validation study is
performed in the three-lepton channel following the 2016 tt̄Z normalization exercise within the
SUSY group. The three-lepton channel is defined by the following selection:

• Pass all filters that remove detector- and beam-related noise as described in Section 4.11.
• Pass the single lepton trigger as described in Section 5.1.3.
• Have exactly 3 leptons passing medium ID requirement
• Leading lepton, which is a muon or electron required by the trigger, with
pT > 50 or 40 GeV respectively
• Second and third leading leptons with pT > 20 GeV
• Have one reconstructed Z boson within the mass window of 81–101 GeV
• Have at least 4 jets with pT > 40 GeV, |η | < 2.4
• Have at least 2 b-tagged jets with pT > 30 GeV, |η | < 2.4
• pmiss
> 30 GeV
T

The tt̄Z and other background yields are normalized to the SM prediction. The measured yields
of tt̄Z and backgrounds are compared with single lepton data as shown in Fig. 48, as a function
of the reconstructed Z boson mass. The observed tt̄Z yield is calculated by subtracting background yields from the data yield. The ratio of the observed and predicted tt̄Z yield is taken
as the tt̄Z normalization scale factor. The statistical uncertainties of numerator and denominator are propagated as the uncertainty of the normalization scale factor. From the Single Muon
dataset, the derived scale factor is 1.06871 ± 0.1589, and from the Single Electron dataset, the
scale factor is 1.44722 ± 0.2091.

Figure 48: tt̄Z validation in the three-lepton channel from Single Muon and Single Electron
data. The error bar denotes the statistical uncertainty.

A recent measurement of the inclusive cross section of top quark pair production in association with a Z boson using proton-proton collisions at a center-of-mass energy of 13 TeV at the
LHC is performed with data corresponding to an integrated luminosity of 77.5 fb−1 , collected
by the CMS experiment during 2016 and 2017 [40]. The measurement is performed using final
states containing three or four charged leptons (electrons or muons), and the Z boson is detected through its decay to an oppositely charged lepton pair. The production cross section is
measured to be 0.95 ± 0.05 (stat.) ± 0.06 (syst.) pb. The tt̄Z scale factor can be calculated from
the measured cross section as 0.95 ± 0.05 ± 0.06/0.7826 = 1.214 ± 0.0997. This scale factor is
in agreement with our measurement above and chosen for the background normalization as it
provided smaller uncertainty.

The dominant systematic uncertainties coming from jet energy scale and unclustered MET in
the tt̄Z MC samples are shown in Fig. 49. Other systematic uncertainties, including pileup correction, b- and soft b-tagging scale factors, prefire weight, PDF weight, MET trigger efficiency,
top- and W-tagging scale factors, and the tt̄Z normalization scale factors are also considered,
as further described in Section 10.

Yields of other SM backgrounds

The yields of remaining SM backgrounds are derived from MC samples normalized to the SM
predicted cross section. A generator-level veto of leptons from hard processes is applied to
avoid double-counting with the lost-lepton backgrounds. These backgrounds are combined as
one rare background.

In this section various categories of systematic uncertainty that affect the analysis are discussed.

Systematic uncertainties
Background prediction uncertainties

Systematic uncertainties common to signal and backgrounds include those from b- and soft
b-tagging scale factors, MET trigger efficiency, pileup reweight, L1 prefire weight, jet energy
scale, corrections from unclustered MET, lepton scale factors, and top- and W-tagging scale
factors. In the case of event reweighting, the differences by varying the corresponding weight
within its systematic uncertainty boundary are taken as the systematic uncertainty. For uncertainties associated with physics objects, events are reevaluated by varying the physics object
quantities within their associated systematic uncertainties boundaries. Each background prediction is carried out in order to account for events shifting to different bins.
Statistical uncertainty of control regions in data: The dominant uncertainty in data-driven background prediction methods is often the result of limited statistics of the control regions
that are used to derive the background estimate for the signal regions, in particular for
search regions with tighter requirements.
Statistical uncertainty of simulated samples: In some cases, the uncertainties on transfer factors derived from simulation also have a significant statistical component due to limited
statistics in the simulated samples.
Uncertainties related to extrapolation from control to signal regions: Each data-driven background estimation method relies on an extrapolation from a control region to the signal
region. This leads to uncertainties specific to each method, related to the nature of the extrapolation. For example, the lost-lepton background prediction strategy uses the region
with a selected lepton to estimate the background yield in the vetoed region. Appropriate correction factors are applied to both selected and vetoed regions to account for
differences in the lepton selection efficiency between data and simulation. The precision
of these corrections affects the uncertainty in the background estimation.
Jet energy scale: The systematic uncertainties associated with the jet energy corrections applied to MC and data samples, following the JetMET POG recipes. The uncertainties are
propagated to both AK4 jets and pmiss
with type-1 corrections.
T
pmiss
unclustered energy: The systematic uncertainties associated with the unclustered energy
T
with pmiss
by varying each PF particle energy by its own resolution as uncertainty [41].
T
b-tagging: The systematic uncertainties associated with the b-tagging efficiency correction factors using the provided SF values from the BTV POG 4.6.1.
Soft-b tagging: The systematic uncertainties associated with the soft b-tagging efficiency correction factor [22], which was taken from the envelope of the data MC differences in the
scale factor fits within a tt̄-enriched region.
Lepton scale factor: The systematic uncertainties associated with the lepton scale factor provided by POGs and the SUSY working group.
Pileup reweighting: MC events are reweighted to the pileup profile of data by following the
LUMI POG recipe. The inelastic proton-proton cross section used is 69.2 mb [42]. The
systematic uncertainty of pileup reweighting is derived by varying the inelastic protonproton cross session with 4.6% variation of the measured cross session.
