1352

ISR jet multiplicity correction: The systematic uncertainties associated with the ISR jet multiplicity corrections from SUSY POG [43]. The ISR jet multiplicity corrections are only
applied for 2016 tt̄ MC and signal samples as suggested. The ISR jet multiplicity uncertainty of tt̄ MC in the lost lepton background is treated uncorrelated from the ISR jet
multiplicity uncertainty of signal to prevent over constraint in the fit.
Level-1 prefiring correction: The systematic uncertainties associated with the L1 prefiring reweighting procedure. The provided upward and downward values of the event weight are
obtained by shifting all prefiring probabilities plus or minus their uncertainties. At the
moment, the uncertainty is taken as the maximum between 20 percent of the object prefiring probability and the statistical uncertainty associated with the considered bin in the
prefiring map.
Uncertainties related to the merged top and W-tagging: The impacts of different effects on the
determination of the scale factors are studied. We followed the recommendation from the
DeepAK8 group [3, 4], which is also described in Section 4.8.
Uncertainties related to the Resolved Top Tagging: A similar list of the sources of systematic
uncertainties is evaluated for the resolved tagger as in the case of merged tops [5, 6],
which is also described in Section 4.9.
PDF uncertainties: The systematic uncertainties associated with the PDF used in the MC generation, by following the PDF4LHC [44] recommendation. It used the mean and standard
deviation for the Gaussian distribution of the eigenvalues from PDF, such as NNPDF, or
the 16th and 84th eigenvalue elements as the uncertainty for PDF with non-Gaussian
distribution. The PDF uncertainty is not applied to signal samples as recommended [45].
Luminosity: A 1.8% uncertainty is assigned on the integrated luminosity measured by CMS
for the Run 2 data-taking period [46]. The prediction of the tt̄Z and rare backgrounds,
which rely on simulation and are normalized based on the measured integrated luminosity, are affected by this uncertainty. The luminosity uncertainty is also assigned to the MC
prediction of background in each control regions and search regions. The estimations of
the SM background processes are data-driven and based on the data observed in control
regions; they are not affected by this uncertainty due to the cancellation between control
regions and search regions.
Renormalization and factorization uncertainties: Variations of renormalization and factorization scales, have a more significant impact on the TTZ and rare background prediction,
which relies almost entirely on simulation, than on the more data-driven background estimates that benefit from a partial or full cancellation of these uncertainties between the
control and signal regions. The envelop of variation of renormalization and factorization
scales are taken as the systematic uncertainty.
In Tables 34, 35, 36 and 37 we have the total uncertainties for the combined background predictions for each search bin. We are only including the dominant systematic uncertainties, but
some backgrounds also have a specific uncertainty attributed to them which is accounted for
in the limits.
Because of the limited statistics of the MC, we have many cases in which our systematic uncertainties are poorly determined for some background or signal in some search bin or control
region unit. We rectify this using a combination of three methods.

10. Systematic uncertainties

1353
1354
1355
1356
1357
1358
1359
1360
1361
1362
1363
1364
1365
1366
1367

97

First, if the statistical uncertainty in the predicted yield in some bin is larger than the predicted
yield, that is if we have more than 100% statistical uncertainty, then all of the systematic uncertainties in that background or signal are ignored in that bin. The rationale for this is that the
systematics cannot be well determined when even the predicted yield is so poorly determined,
and also that, even if we could determine the systematics adequately, the statistical uncertainty
would dominate, rendering the systematics negligible.
Even after this first procedure, we still find some cases in which the systematics are poorly determined even though the predicted yield is well determined. These cases tend to arise from the
JES or pmiss
resolution systematics, which involve altering the kinematic variables in the simuT
lated events and not merely changing the weights of the simulated events. Systematics in this
case can be poorly statistically determined even though the central yield is well determined.
In these cases, the common symptom is that the systematic becomes extremely asymmetric because one direction of variation is dominated by a fluctuation of no more than a few simulated
events. We detect these extremely-asymmetric systematics by looking at the difference of the
absolute value of the natural logarithm of the systematic ratios

|(|ln R(Up)|) − (|ln R(Down)|)| > 0.35,
1368
1369
1370
1371
1372
1373
1374
1375
1376
1377
1378
1379
1380
1381
1382
1383
1384
1385
1386

where R(Up) is the ratio of the predicted yield for the “Up” systematic variation to the unvaried predicted yield, R(Down) is the ratio of the predicted yield for the “Down” systematic
variation to the unvaried predicted yield, and 0.35 is a somewhat arbitrary threshold. When
this threshold is exceeded, we ignore the direction that results in the larger variation based on
the absolute value of the logarithm of the ratio, and replace it with the reciprocal of the remaining ratio. For example, suppose R(Up) = 0.55 and R(Down) = 1.25. Their natural logarithms
are ln R(Up) = −0.60 and ln R(Down) = 0.22, and the difference in the absolute values is 0.37,
which exceeds our threshold. Because −0.60 has a larger absolute value than 0.22, we leave
R(Down) unchanged, and replace R(Up) = 1/R(Down) = 0.8. Note that we use the logarithm of the ratios for our checks and fixes because our systematic uncertainties are modeled
using the log-normal distribution, so the logarithm of the ratio is Gaussian distributed.
Finally, even after the first two procedures, we still find some cases in which both the “Up”
and “Down” variations fall on the same side of the central prediction. That is, both are greater
than the central prediction, or both are less than the central prediction. These one-sided systematic uncertainties cause some technical difficulties. To avoid these technical difficulties, we
symmetrize these cases. Because the systematics are modeled using the log-normal distribution, we divide both “Up” and “Down” systematic ratios by the geometric mean of the two
ratios. This symmetrizes the log-normal distribution and centers it on the predicted yield with
systematic variations.

1388

After these three steps, the systematics are much better behaved and do not cause further technical issues.

Signal uncertainties

Systematic uncertainties unique to signal mostly come from the fullsim/fastsim scale factors,
MET uncertainty in fastsim samples, and ISR jet multiplicity reweighting. Additional fullsim/fastsim scale factors are applied for signal samples generated with the fastsim package.
Fullsim/fastsim scale factors include soft-b, b-tagging, boosted top- and W-tagging, and resolved top tagging. With the scale factors above, the correction factors for each type of object
are varied independently within their uncertainties and propagated to the search bins.

1420

Statistical uncertainty of simulated samples: The predicted signal yields are affected by the
statistical uncertainty in the simulated samples.
Uncertainty on initial state radiation: To improve on the MadGraph modeling of the multiplicity of additional jets from initial state radiation (ISR), simulated signal events are
reweighted based on the number of ISR jets (NJISR ) so as to make the jet multiplicity agree
with data.
Fastsim MET uncertainty: The MET uncertainty here refers to the way MET is recalculated for
fastsim signals. MET is set to the average of generated MET and reconstructed MET, and
the uncertainty is the difference between the reconstructed MET and the new recalculated
value.
Renormalization and factorization uncertainties: Variations of renormalization and factorization scales is taken as systematic uncertainty on the signal. The systematic is taken as a
shape uncertainty, ignoring the acceptance effect from the variation. This systematic uncertainty is treated decorrelated with the renormalization and factorization uncertainty
of TTZ and Rare backgrounds.
Other experimental uncertainties: The uncertainties related to b-tagging, jet energy scale and
resolution, lepton vetoes, and pileup reweighting described above also affect the signal
prediction. In some cases, such as in the description of b-tagging, the fast simulation
used for the signal samples shows some discrepancies with respect to the full simulation.
Additional scale factors are assigned in these cases, and the uncertainties are propagated
with the initial uncertainties.
The systematic uncertainties from different sources are summarized in Table 38 for various
signal models. The systematic uncertainties coming from ISR jet multiplicity reweighting and
pmiss
unclustered energy of a selected T2tt signal point with stop mass of 850 and LSP mass of
T
100 are shown in Fig. 50, as a function of search bins.

Figure 50: The relative systematic uncertainties coming from ISR jet multiplicity reweighting
and pmiss
unclustered energy of the selected T2tt signal point with stop mass of 850 and LSP
T
mass of 100 as a function of search bins.

Results and interpretation
Results
Validation bin yield

In order to test and validate the background estimation strategy, we carry out the background
estimation methods in validation bins, as defined in Section 5.4, to check the agreement between data and the background predictions. The validation region has larger statistics than the
search region and is expected to be signal-depleted.

Interpretation
Statistical analysis

This section describes the statistical interpretation of the selected events in the search regions
defined in Section 5.3.
The statistical interpretation of the results in terms of exclusion limits for the signal models
considered is based on a binned likelihood fit to the observed data taking into account the predicted SM background and signal yields in each bin. SM background predictions for events
from top and W decays (lost-lepton background), Z → νν̄, QCD multijet, tt̄Z, and other processes are made according to the methods described in sections 6, 7, 8, and 9. The extraction of
exclusion limits is based on a modified frequentist approach [47] under the asymptotic approximation [48] and used in conjunction with the CLs criterion described in Refs. [49, 50]. All 183
search region bins, as well as the corresponding single-lepton control region bins, the γ+jets
control region bins, and the QCD multijet background control regions, are fit simultaneously

in order to evaluate the cross section excluded at 95% confidence level for each signal benchmark point. Signal models for which the 95% upper limit on the production cross section falls
below the theoretical cross section (based on approximate-NNLO+NNLL calculations [51–62])
are considered to be excluded by the analysis.
The control regions are included in the likelihood fit and the rate is linked to the corresponding process in the search regions and fit simultaneously. The likelihood function is given by a
product of probability density functions, one for each “bin”. The implementation of this approach utilizes the Higgs Combination tool [63], defines additional bins (channels) for control
regions as well as for signal regions, and use rateParam for linking the control region to the
corresponding process in the search region [64]. The systematic uncertainties assigned to the
signal and background predictions are treated as nuisance parameters in the fit. This statistical

background MC samples in the photon and QCD control regions. The RLL
Z,shape , and RQCD
are the rate parameters that play the role of the transfer factors and propagate the information
from control region fits to the corresponding signal regions. Please note that MC event yields in
i
the γ CR are normalized to data in each photon CR category defined in (Nb ,Nj ), so that RZ,shape
Z →`` CR

works as the shape correction, and RZ,norm. in the above equation is the normalization factor
RZ discussed in Section 7 and sets the normalization of the Z → νν̄ background. As discussed
in the background estimation sections of Sections 6–8, the control regions are not binned in Nt ,
NW , and Nres , and the predictions are “extrapolated” to the SR with the inclusion of the top
and W tags, along with scale factors. This extrapolation is achived by having Nt , Nres , and
NW dependence in the search region terms, but not in the control region terms in the above
equation. The actual implementation is further complicated because search region binnings in
pmiss
and HT are not uniform for different Nb , Nt , Nres , and NW bins. This complication is taken
T
care of by using multiple control regions and associated rate parameters for a single search
region.
LLCR are estimated signal yields based on the SUSY cross sections from the
SR
and Nsignal
The Nsignal
state-of-the-art approximate NNLO+NNLL calculations. The inclusion of the lost-lepton control region (LLCR) in the fit allows us to account for potential signal contamination in the
control region based on the corresponding expected signal yields in the LLCR for any given
signal model. For signal mass points near our expected exclusion limits at high masses, the signal contamination is generally less than 1% of the expected background yields in our control
regions. The main phase space where the signal contamination becomes significant is for T2tt
models where ∆m(et , χe01 ) ∼ m(mt ) and low mass T2bW, and T2tb models. The other commonly
used method to account for the signal contamination is to reduce the signal event yields in the
signal region based on the expected signal contamination in the LLCR by
SR 0

Nsignal
= Nsignal
− TFLL · Nsignal
.

1543

This method treats the amount by which the lost lepton background would be overestimated
as a reduction in signal efficiency. These two approaches give nearly identical results when
the signal contamination is not significant. For signals with low ∆m signals, the latter gives
more conservative limits, i.e. the higher cross section limit by up to 50%. In order to stay on the
conservative side for treating the signal contamination, we adopt the latter approach for results
presented below.
Statistical uncertainties due to the limited statistics of simulated samples are treated uncorrelated between all regions. We adopted the autoMCStats feature of the Higgs combine tool
with autoMCStats 10 1 1. This means that, if the effective number of unweighted MC
events is above 10, the MC statistical uncertainties are modeled with the Barlow-Beeston-lite
approach, which means that we assign a single nuisance parameter to scale the sum of the different process MC yields in each bin. Below the threshold of 10, separate nuisance parameters,
one per process, are used.
The experimental uncertainties related to the lepton and tau ID/veto, b-tagging, soft b-tagging,
jet energy scale, MET clustered energy, pileup reweighting, and the top- and W-tagging are
correlated across all search regions and for all backgrounds. The uncertainty on the Z → νν̄
prediction due to the uncertainties on RZ is correlated separately for each Nb (and NSV ) category, and the uncertainty due to the difference between Z and γ is correlated separately among
search regions of low ∆m and high ∆m categories. The uncertainties on the QCD background
corresponding to the closure of the smearing, r jet correction, and the fake MET event trigger ef-

1545

ficiency uncertainty applicable to QCD multijet MC samples are treated correlated for all search
regions.

11.2.3

Expected limits

Figure 62 shows the 95% confidence level (CL) upper limits on the direct top squark pair production cross section in the context of the T2tt, T2bW, and T2tb models. The upper limits are
used to derive an exclusion region in the met –mχ̃0 plane by comparison to the theoretical cross
1
sections calculated at approximate NNLO+NNLL in Refs. [65–68]. For the T2tt model, top
squark masses up to 1310 GeV and LSP masses up to 640 GeV are excluded. For the T2tt model
we do not present cross section upper limits in the region of |met − mt − mχ̃0 | < 25 GeV and
1
met < 275 GeV as shown in Fig. 62 (upper left). In this region, signal events become similar to
SM tt̄ events and the signal acceptance changes rapidly and is very sensitive to the details of
the simulation, so no interpretation is presented. For the T2bW and T2tb models, top squark
masses up to 1170 GeV and 1150 GeV and LSP masses up to 550 GeV and 500 GeV are excluded,
respectively. For the T2bW and T2tb models, there are regions of low met and mχ̃0 where signals
1
are not excluded by the observed limits at the 95% CL. The sensitivity is reduced in this region
because the top squark decay products have low pT and the signal acceptance becomes smaller.
Figure 63 shows the 95% CL upper limits on the production cross section in the plane of met
versus ∆m = met − mχ̃0 for the T2ttC, T2bwC, and T2cc models. Signal events with ∆m below

mW in the range of 10–80 GeV are considered. Top squark masses up to 640 GeV are excluded
at the 95% CL in the context of the T2ttC model, 740 GeV for the T2bWC model, and 630 GeV
for the T2cc model.
Exclusion limits for the models of the gluino pair production, T1tttt, T1ttbb, and T5ttcc, are
shown in the mge –mχ̃0 plane in Figs. 64 and 65. Gluino masses up to 2260 GeV and LSP masses
1
up to 1410 GeV are excluded for the T1tttt model, with corresponding limits of 2250 and 1400 GeV
for the T1ttbb model, and 2150 and 1380 GeV for the T5ttcc model. In the case of the T5ttcc
model there is a reduction in the sensitivity as the mχ̃0 approaches zero. This is due to the
1
kinematic properties of the top squark decay et → c χ̃01 . The LSP in this situation carries only
a small fraction of the top squark momentum, and this results in reduced pmiss
and reduced
T
signal acceptance.
With the SUSY particle spectrum assumed in the T5ttcc model, direct top squark production
should also occur as in the T2cc model. For mχ̃0 < 600 GeV, the T2cc model is excluded by this
1
search and by earlier searches by the ATLAS [? ] and CMS [? ? ? ] experiments as well as by
the LEP experiments [69–72]. For mχ̃0 > 600 GeV where the T2cc model is not excluded, adding
1
the T2cc direct top squark production contributions to the gluino pair production contributions
already present in T5ttcc does not have a significant effect on the sensitivity. For simplicity,
Fig. 65 shows the exclusion based on the T5ttcc model without contributions from direct top
squark production. The exclusions based on T2cc are shown separately.

Figure 65: The 95% CL upper limit on the production cross section of the T5ttcc simplified
model as a function of the gluino and LSP masses. The solid black curves represent the observed exclusion contour with respect to approximate NNLO+NNLL signal cross sections and
the change in this contour due to variation of these cross sections within their theoretical uncertainties [51–54, 56–62]. The dashed red curves indicate the mean expected exclusion contour

1609

12

Conclusion

Results are presented from a search for direct and gluino-mediated top squark production in
proton-proton collisions at a center-of-mass energy of 13 TeV. Features of interest in the analysis
include tagging algorithms for top quarks and W bosons both at low and high pT . The search is
based on events with at least two jets and large imbalance in transverse momentum pmiss
T . The
−1
data set corresponds to an integrated luminosity of 137 fb collected with the CMS detector
at the CERN LHC during the years 2016–2018. A set of 183 search regions is defined based on
several kinematic variables and the number of reconstructed top quarks, bottom quarks, and
W bosons. No statistically significant excess of events is observed relative to the expectation
from the standard model.

1616

Upper limits at 95% confidence level are established on the cross section for several simplified
models of top squark pair production as a function of the masses of the SUSY particles. Using
the calculated cross sections, lower limits at 95% confidence level are established on the masses
of the top squark, the LSP, and the gluino. The limit on the T2tt model reaches up to 1.3 TeV for
the stop mass with the LSP mass at 1 GeV, while the limit on the T1tttt model reaches 2.2 TeV
for the gluino mass with the LSP mass at 1 GeV. These results significantly extend those of
previous searches.

1617

Search bins for high ∆m

After the high ∆m baseline selection discussed in Section 5.2, we then divide the remaining
phase space of high ∆m into search bins in order to provide the best sensitivity for high ∆m
signals with different mass points.
The primary binning variables are those which represent the topologies of the signals, i.e. number of fully merged top, W and resolved top, number of jets and b jets and pmiss

separated peaks in both of these two plots. Hence, binning with these two variables provides
good discriminating power. Figure 66 shows the number of jets (left) and b jets (right) distributions when mbT > 175 GeV. The signals are scaled to the sum of the backgrounds in the upper
pad for a better view.

Search bin optimization

After setting up the search bin study framework as described above, we then have performed
several rounds of search bin optimization, including choosing the working points for our taggers, checking if it is necessary to add/merge binning variables and add/merge search bins.
All these changes have sophisticated effects and it is hard to make a decision by just thinking.
Hence, we choose to base our decisions on expected limits, which are calculated with simplified
data cards.
As mentioned in Section 11, real exclusion limits are calculated with real data and either data
driven or data corrected background predictions with elaborate systematic uncertainties. However, the data for this analysis is still blinded and the full systematics take too much computing
resources to give prompt feedbacks of the effects of a change. Therefore, simplified data cards
are used instead for search bin optimization.
In simplified data cards, the observed data are set to be the sum of background predictions
which are MC driven, given that they are very close to the real data driven predictions in the
validation regions.
Here are more details of the background prediction in simplified data cards. The prediction
of the lost lepton background, which is the dominant background in high ∆m, is obtained by
emulating the translation factor method where the translation factor is calculated with MC and
the corresponding statistical uncertainty is treated as a systematic uncertainty. The “data” in
the control region is also estimated based on MC on which we apply a global normalization
by a ratio of 2958/3378, which is the ratio of data and MC yields in the control region of our
previous analysis. We also apply a global normalization on the Z(νν) + jets background, which
is the dominant background in low ∆m, by a ratio of 667/852, which is also from our previous
analysis. Since all the rest backgrounds contribute much less, their predictions come directly
from the MC yields.
Then we assign flat values of systematic uncertainties to each background in high ∆m, which
is from CMS-SUS-16-050 [2] and low ∆m, which is from CMS-SUS-16-049 [1].

1846

Figure 69 is the simplified data card of search bin number 68 for a T2tt signal mass point of
m(t̃) = 1300 GeV and m(χ̃01 ) = 50 GeV, calculated with full Run 2 MC. For a certain mass point,
each search bin has its own data card like this and we combine all the 183 search bins together
to calculate the limits of that mass point.

1847

In this data card, we have three channels: ch1, ch2 and ch3, which refer to the search region,

single electron control region, and single muon control region, respectively. The “observation”
refers to the data and we set it to be the background sum as described above. Note that the
Higgs Combine Tool supports float values for the number of data. That’s why the data in the
SR is a float number. Then we have a row of processes where signal is labeled with 0 and the
backgrounds are labeled with ascending positive integers. Below the process, we have a row
of rate, which refers to the MC driven prediction of each process.
Last, we have uncertainties. Note that the bin number is “chn69” here, because Root counts the
bins from 1 while our search bin indexes start from 0. The statistical uncertainties are handled
by gamma distributions (gmN) for the predictions that directly come from MC, where the first
parameter, which is an integer, is the number of effective events and the second parameter
in the same row is the average weight. It is inevitable that some minor backgrounds such as
QCD have no MC events at all in some tail (tight) bins of ≥ 3 b jets/ tops. In these situations,
the number of effective events is 0 and the average weight is set to be an average of the nonzero tail bins’. For the predictions using the translation factor method, such as the lost-lepton
background, we choose log-uniform distributions (lnU) to handle their statistical uncertainties.
A log-uniform distribution has one parameter k, which allows the corresponding prediction to
float freely from central value /k to central value ×k. We choose a conservative value of 10 to
the parameter k.
The systematic uncertainties are either calculated, such as the uncertainty of the translation
factor, or assigned flat values as described above.
In term of the search bin optimization, we first studied the working points of our taggers. We
found that tight working points (tight WP for all AK8 top, W and resolved top taggers) give
the best limits to T2tt signal models while the medium WP of resolved top tagger gives the best
limits to T1tttt signal models and the loose WP of W tagger gives the best limits to T2bW signal
models. Since we consider that T2tt is our most important target, we decided to use tight WP
(see Sections 4.8 and 4.9).
Then we checked if it is necessary to add/merge search bin variables. The mT2 variable was
proved to be powerful for pair production signals, such as the T2tt signal model in our previous

B. Tau identification studies

1876
1877
1878
1879
1880
1881
1882
1883
1884
1885

135

analysis. So we tried both using the mT2 as a baseline cut and using it as a binning variable. Unfortunately, it only improved the limits by a few percents so we decided to drop it for simplicity.
The HT variable is well known for being very powerful to gluino mediated signal models such
as T1tttt, so we applied it as a binning variable in ≥ 3 b jets/ tops bins. Then we found that
it also improves the sensitivity of T2tt, so we added the HT binning variable to 1–2 b jets/ top
bins as well.
We also checked if it helps to merge the three binning variables of Nt , NW , and Nres into one
variable. The expected limits showed that it has little impact on T1tttt signal models but degrades the T2tt and T2bW signal models. Therefore, we decided to keep these three binning
variables separated.

1888

Last, we merged search bins with too few backgrounds and make sure each search bin has
non-zero lost lepton prediction, which led to our final search bin design with 53 bins for low
∆m and 130 bins for high ∆m as shown in the precious sections.

1887

Tau identification studies

The identification of taus that decay hadronically has been under extensive study to distinguish
whether the custom tau multivariate analysis (tauMVA), the isolated track (isotrack) method,
or the MVA from that is provided by the tau POG (tauPOG) has the highest efficiency to fake
rate. The custom tauMVA was trained on PF charged hadron candidates with pT > 10 GeV and
|η | < 2.4 along with an additional PF photon candidate, if any, with pT > 5 GeV and within a
cone of ∆R ≤ 0.2 of the charged hadron candidate. The tau candidate is also required to have
miss
a transverse mass m T (τh , pmiss
T ) < 100 GeV, where m T ( τh , pT ) is defined as follows,
q
m T (τh , pmiss
)
=
2 · pT (τh + nearestγ) · pmiss
· (1 − cos ∆φ).
(15)
T
T
1890
1891
1892
1893
1894
1895
1896
1897
1898
1899
1900
1901
1902
1903
1904
1905
1906
1907
1908
1909

The addition of photons in the final definition is due to the possibility of taus decaying to
neutral pions. This improves the resolution for the hadronic tau candidate. The inputs for the
MVA are as follows:

• The pT and |η | of the τ candidate.
• The sum pT of charged particles associated to the primary vertex within ∆R cones of
sizes 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, and 0.4 around the τ candidate.
• The summed pT of all particles within ∆R cones of sizes 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, and 0.4 around
the candidate, now including the neutral contribution from pileup particles, which
is reduced by applying the ∆β correction to the neutral component of the isolation
quantity.
• The distance in ∆R to the nearest charged PF candidate with pT > 1 GeV.
• The distance in ∆R to the axis of the jet containing the τ candidate, and the b-tagging
discriminant (DeepCSV) value for the jet, provided that the jet has pT > 30 GeV and
|η | < 2.4.
In Figure 70, we have a Receiver Operator Characteristic curve to compare the efficiency of the
TauMVA in identifying taus that have been gen-level matched and the fake rate of tagging a
parton as a tau that has not been gen-level matched. This shows that the TauMVA can potentially be a good discriminator of vetoing taus in our SM background while also not vetoing
potential signal events. Then in Fig. 71, we compare the TauMVA discriminator for events
that have been matched to a gen-level tau (solid) and those that are not matched (dotted). We

Merged top/W misidentification scale factors

The misidentification rate for the merged top taggers is important for this analysis as both
the QCD and Z → νν̄ backgrounds contain only fake tops and Ws. The estimation of the
misidentification rate scale factors is straight forward and follows the general strategy from [4].
We choose a γ + jets data control region for the measurement of the SF because this will give a
closer match to the larger Z → νν̄ background. Because of differences between the estimates of
the SF seen in [4] between γ + jets and QCD control regions (attributed to differences in quark
and gluon content of jets in these samples), we apply an additional systematic uncertainty of
5% to our SF to cover any differences between samples. The γ + jets control region is defined
using the following selection

•
•
•
•

At least 1 photon with pT > 220 GeV
HT > 300 GeV
Pass the electron, muon, tau, and isotrack veto’s as defined in subsection 5.2
In 2018 the HEM veto for jets, electrons, and photons are applied as described in
subsection 4.13

• Data events are required to pass the single photon trigger with a 200 GeV threshold
In addition to the selection, we reweight the MC events to account for data versus simulation differences in the number of b-tagged jets, the pileup spectrum, and the photon trigger
efficiency. This selection gives a CR which is dominated by γ + jets events with small contamination from QCD, and negligible contributions from real tops. After top/W selections are
applied we see that the fraction from events with real tops increases to about 10%. The results
for the SF calculated ignoring this contamination versus subtracting the contamination from
data and MC is found to be negligible and the effect is ignored.
The highest pT AK8 jet separated from the high pT photons by at least ∆R > 1.0 in each event
is selected to act as the top/W proxy in each event. The tagging rate (misidentification rate) is
then defined as the fraction of the selected proxy top/Ws which pass the top/W identification
criterion as defined in subsection 4.8. The SF itself is defined from the ratio of the tagging rate
in data versus simulation. The SFs are shown in Figure 72 and Figure 73.
The top tagging misidentification SF are limited by their statistical uncertainty but within those
uncertainties are reasonably flat with pT . Because these SF are consistent with a flat SF within
their uncertainties, as are those calculated in [4] for a tighter working point, we instead will
use a flat SF for the top misidentification SF. The final scale factors used along with their uncertainties (including statistical plus the additional 5% from differences in samples) is shown
in Table 51 and Table 52.
It is interesting to note the distribution of the number of generator partons reconstructed inside
each fake tagged top. We define the number of generator partons as any prompt quark (except
top) or gluon which has at least 10 GeV pT and is within ∆R < 0.6 of the AK8 jet. Figure 74

shows the distribution of number of matched generator partons for AK8 jets which are tagged
as top quarks for tt̄, γ + jets, and Z → νν̄ MC. While the ttbar sample, which is dominated

Top pT reweighting

The background estimation for the lost lepton SM uses a transfer factor approach with additional extrapolation in top and W tags for the high ∆m control region. There are some observed
differences when the predictions using the extrapolation and without extrapolation methods.
This is most likely due to mismodelling of the top pT spectrum, as observed in the CMS tt̄
differential cross section measurements [36, 37].
We explored a few options to account for this effect [33] such as, correction based on the 4-vector
sum of leptons, b-quarks, and pmiss
( pT (l, b, pmiss
T
T )), which should correlate to a leptonically
decaying top pT in the lost lepton control region, see Fig. 75. Three separate corrections using
gen
pT tops are performed: (1) a data to POWHEG correction [36, 37] (Fig. 76 top), (2) a POWHEG to
M AD G RAPH correction (Fig. 76 middle row), and also (3) a combination of the two. In Fig. 76,
the middle left and right plots show the comparison between the POWHEG and M AD G RAPH
gen
top pT distributions for 2017 and 2018, respectively while the figure on top is the fit for data
to POWHEG. A correction using the leading AK8 jet pT as a data to MC correction for all of the
tt̄ MC or only for Ak8 jet matched to top tags has also been tested.
In Fig. 77, we have the Nt distributions for each method of the top pT correction/reweighting.
On the top left, is the distribution without any top pT correction with a large downward trend
in the ration and the top right is the correction with the p T (l, b, pmiss
which only
T ) correction
gen
slightly reduces the downward trend. In the middle, we have the three separte pT corrections:
Top pT , Top POWHEG/MG, and top pT + POWHEG/MG which is a combination of the first two.
The latter correction significantly improves the ratio. Finally, on the bottom, is the reweighting
using the leading AK8 jet: AK8 jet matched to gen top (Top AK8 Matching), and any AK8
jet without any matching (Top AK8 Full). The far right plot on the bottom is showing the
separated out tt̄ MC with matches to tops, W, and other (fakes, mistags). From these plots,
we fit the ratio to find the methods that should be investigated further. The two methods
are the top pT + POWHEG/MG and Top AK8 Full. In Fig. 76 (bottom), we also have the AK8

jet pT that is matched to a top tag. We include distributions with and without the top pT +
POWHEG /MG reweighting. We see significant improvement in the agreement between data
and MC with the top pT + POWHEG/MG eweighting and any residual disagreement is covered
by the uncertainties.
The investigations for the top pT correction were motivated by the high ∆m validation bins. So
now we look at the Nt , NW , and Nres distributions and then the predictions with each method.
In Fig. 78, we show these distributions for high ∆m bin 27 in the validation region, see Table 18.
Due to the extrapolations in the lost lepton control region, this bin also corresponds to the control region of bins 29, 31, and 33. Figure 78 shows the pmiss
(top), Nt (middle), and NW (bottom)
T
distributions for the two top pT corrections. We see that both these corrections improve the
downward trend in the ratio. These are fitted with a line and are within 1 sigma of each other,
so we want to check how the extrapolation comparisons change with each of these corrections.
Both the top pT + POWHEG/MG and the Top Ak8 Full methods show promising improvement
in the data to MC agreement for Nt , NW , and Nres . Now we take both of these corrections
and get the lost lepton prediction with and without extrapolations in the high ∆m validation
regions. In Fig. 79, we have the prediction with and without the extrapolation methods for
each method: No Top Weight, top pT + POWHEG/MG, and Top AK8 full. For each of the new
top pT weights, we also calculate the systematics for each method. We also include the pulls
for each method, the mean and sigma has improved for each method, but is comparable for
both. Now since each method provides a similarly good correction for the predictions we need
to consider the down sides. The top pT + POWHEG/MG method was successfully adopted for
this search using the 2016 data. For the full Run 2 analysis the recommendation by the TOP
PAG is not to use blindly the top-pT reweighting, mainly because of the concern of potential
signal contamination effect on the top pT spectrum measurement. We have studied the signal
contamination in the lost lepton high ∆m control region, corresponding to the phase space
where the top pT spectrum measurement is performed, and it is less than 1% for select signal
points near the expected exclusion boundary. The problem with the Top AK8 Full method is
that is does not use an unfolded top quark pT spectrum for the correction. Since the correction is
applied to all of tt̄ MC even if the leading Ak8 jet is not matched to a top, this correction is valid
only if we perform a full study that includes an unfolding of the leading AK8 pT distribution
to the top quark pT distribution, which requires significant additional analyses. Therefore, we
have decided to include the top pT + POWHEG/MG correction for tt̄ MC, see Section 6.

E

Soft b efficiencies

As one of the binning variables in this analysis is the number of soft b jets, the efficiency of soft
b tagging is important for this analysis. The targets for soft b tagging are low pT b quarks that
have decayed from a top quark. Calculating the efficiency then requires matching secondary
vertices to generator-level partons in simulated Monte Carlo data. A secondary vertex and a
generator-level parton are considered matched when ∆R < 0.4. Only generator-level partons
that are the last copy of themselves in the event, are outgoing particles of the hardest process,
and decay from a top quark, W boson, or gluon splitting are considered. The denominator of
the efficiency is the set of secondary vertices that are matched to a generator-level b quark with
the above requirements, and the numerator is the subset of those secondary vertices that pass
the soft b requirements:

•
•
•
•
•

|IP2D| < 3
SIP3D > 4
cos(SV-PV, p) > 0.98
Ntracks > 2
pIVF
T < 20 GeV

2045

No event selections are applied.

2046

Sometimes these requirements are satisfied by secondary vertices that match to other generatorlevel partons. The efficiency is also measured for c quark matching, and the fake rate is calculated for those secondary vertices that match u, d, or s quarks or gluons. The same limitations
on generator-level partons are used for these calculations; the extension to other partons is
why partons decaying from W bosons or gluon splitting are allowed. The denominator then
changes to the set of secondary vertices that match one of the other parton groups as shown in
Figure 81 and Figure 82, while the numerator remains the subset of secondary vertices in the
denominator that also pass the soft b requirements.

The following efficiencies were calculated using TTToSemiLeptonic samples for each year. The
b quark tagging efficiency, c quark tagging efficiency, and fake rate as functions of secondary
vertex pT are shown in Figure 80, Figure 81, and Figure 82. The b-quark efficiency from soft-b
tagging is found to be 39-41% on average. The charm efficiency is found to be 17-20%, and the
fake rate is found to be 2-5%.
